<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Galaxy</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the astronomical structure. For our galaxy, see <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Galaxy_(disambiguation)" title="Galaxy (disambiguation)">Galaxy (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:314px;max-width:314px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:312px;max-width:312px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NGC_4414_(NASA-med).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1428" data-file-width="1730" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/NGC_4414_%28NASA-med%29.jpg/310px-NGC_4414_%28NASA-med%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/NGC_4414_%28NASA-med%29.jpg/465px-NGC_4414_%28NASA-med%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/NGC_4414_%28NASA-med%29.jpg/620px-NGC_4414_%28NASA-med%29.jpg 2x" width="310"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/NGC_4414" title="NGC 4414">NGC 4414</a>, a typical <a href="/wiki/Spiral_galaxy" title="Spiral galaxy">spiral galaxy</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Constellation" title="Constellation">constellation</a> <a href="/wiki/Coma_Berenices" title="Coma Berenices">Coma Berenices</a>, is about 55,000 <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a> in diameter and approximately 60 million light-years from Earth.</div></div></div></div>
<p>A <b>galaxy</b> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">gravitationally</a> bound system of <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stellar_remnant" title="Stellar remnant">stellar remnants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar gas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">dust</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sparkegallagher2000_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-sparkegallagher2000-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa060812_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa060812-2">[2]</a></sup> The word galaxy is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> <i><i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek-language transliteration">galaxias</i></i> (<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">γαλαξίας</span>), literally "milky", a reference to the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>. Galaxies range in size from <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_galaxy" title="Dwarf galaxy">dwarfs</a> with just a few hundred million (10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">8</span></sup>) stars to <a href="/wiki/IC_1101" title="IC 1101">giants</a> with one hundred <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_(numbers)#1012" title="Orders of magnitude (numbers)">trillion</a> (10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">14</span></sup>) stars,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science250_4980_539_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-science250_4980_539-3">[3]</a></sup> each orbiting its galaxy's <a href="/wiki/Center_of_mass" title="Center of mass">center of mass</a>.
</p><p>Galaxies are categorized according to their visual morphology as <a href="/wiki/Elliptical_galaxy" title="Elliptical galaxy">elliptical</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uf030616_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-uf030616-4">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Spiral_galaxy" title="Spiral galaxy">spiral</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Irregular_galaxy" title="Irregular galaxy">irregular</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IRatlas_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-IRatlas-5">[5]</a></sup> Many galaxies are thought to have <a href="/wiki/Supermassive_black_hole" title="Supermassive black hole">supermassive black holes</a> at their centers. The Milky Way's central black hole, known as <a href="/wiki/Sagittarius_A*" title="Sagittarius A*">Sagittarius A*</a>, has a mass four million times greater than the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smbh_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-smbh-6">[6]</a></sup> As of March 2016, <a href="/wiki/GN-z11" title="GN-z11">GN-z11</a> is the oldest and most distant observed galaxy with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comoving_distance" title="Comoving distance">comoving distance</a> of 32 billion <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light-years" title="Light-years">light-years</a> from Earth, and observed as it existed just 400 million years after the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>.
</p><p>Research released in 2016 revised the number of galaxies in the <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">observable universe</a> from a previous estimate of 200 billion (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7011200000000000000♠</span>2<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>11</sup></span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apj624_2_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-apj624_2-7">[7]</a></sup> to a suggested 2 trillion (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7012200000000000000♠</span>2<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>12</sup></span>) or more,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Conselice_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Conselice-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20161017_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20161017-9">[9]</a></sup> containing more <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> than all the <a href="/wiki/Sand" title="Sand">grains of sand</a> on planet <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SU-20020201_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-SU-20020201-10">[10]</a></sup> Most of the galaxies are 1,000 to 100,000 <a href="/wiki/Parsec" title="Parsec">parsecs</a> in diameter (approximately 3000 to 300,000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_year" title="Light year">light years</a>) and separated by distances on the order of millions of parsecs (or megaparsecs).  For comparison, the Milky Way has a diameter of at least 30,000 parsecs (100,000 LY) and is separated from the <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy" title="Andromeda Galaxy">Andromeda Galaxy</a>, its nearest large neighbor, by 780,000 parsecs (2.5 million LY).
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intergalactic_space" title="Intergalactic space">space</a> between galaxies is filled with a tenuous gas (the <a href="/wiki/Outer_space#Intergalactic_space" title="Outer space">intergalactic medium</a>) having an average density of less than one <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atom</a> per cubic meter. The majority of galaxies are gravitationally organized into <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_group" title="Galaxy group">groups</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_cluster" title="Galaxy cluster">clusters</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Supercluster" title="Supercluster">superclusters</a>. The Milky Way is part of the <a href="/wiki/Local_Group" title="Local Group">Local Group</a>, which is dominated by it and the Andromeda Galaxy and is part of the <a href="/wiki/Virgo_Supercluster" title="Virgo Supercluster">Virgo Supercluster</a>.  At the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Large-scale_structure_of_the_Cosmos" title="Large-scale structure of the Cosmos">largest scale</a>, these associations are generally arranged into <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_filament" title="Galaxy filament">sheets and filaments</a> surrounded by immense <a href="/wiki/Void_(astronomy)" title="Void (astronomy)">voids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-camb_lss_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-camb_lss-11">[11]</a></sup>  The largest structure of galaxies yet recognised is a cluster of superclusters that has been named <a href="/wiki/Laniakea_Supercluster" title="Laniakea Supercluster">Laniakea</a>, which contains the Virgo supercluster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-38"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-39"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The origin of the word <i>galaxy</i> derives from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> term for the Milky Way, <i><i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek-language transliteration">galaxias</i></i> (<i><span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%B3%CE%B1%CE%BB%CE%B1%CE%BE%CE%AF%CE%B1%CF%82" title="wikt:γαλαξίας">γαλαξίας</a></span></i>, "milky one"), or <i><i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek-language transliteration"><a href="/wiki/Kyklos" title="Kyklos">kyklos</a> galaktikos</i></i> ("milky circle")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oed_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-oed-13">[13]</a></sup> due to its appearance as a "milky" band of light in the sky. In <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a> places his son born by a mortal woman, the infant <a href="/wiki/Heracles" title="Heracles">Heracles</a>, on <a href="/wiki/Hera" title="Hera">Hera</a>'s breast while she is asleep so that the baby will drink her divine milk and will thus become immortal. Hera wakes up while breastfeeding and then realizes she is nursing an unknown baby: she pushes the baby away, some of her milk spills, and it produces the faint band of light known as the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-waller_hodge2003_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-waller_hodge2003-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-konecny2006_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-konecny2006-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the astronomical literature, the capitalized word "Galaxy" is often used to refer to our galaxy, the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>, to distinguish it from the other galaxies in our <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>. The English term <i>Milky Way</i> can be traced back to a story by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaucer" title="Chaucer">Chaucer</a> <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1380</span>:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>"See yonder, lo, the Galaxyë<br/> Which men <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/clepe" title="wikt:clepe">clepeth</a> <i>the Milky Wey</i>,<br/> For hit is whyt."</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Geoffrey Chaucer, <a href="/wiki/The_House_of_Fame" title="The House of Fame">The House of Fame</a><i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oed_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-oed-13">[13]</a></sup></i></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Galaxies were initially discovered telescopically and were known as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spiral_nebula" title="Spiral nebula">spiral nebulae</a></i>. Most 18th to 19th Century astronomers considered them as either unresolved <a href="/wiki/Star_cluster" title="Star cluster">star clusters</a> or anagalactic <a href="/wiki/Nebula" title="Nebula">nebulae</a>, and were just thought as a part of the Milky Way, but their true composition and natures remained a mystery. Observations using larger telescopes of a few nearby bright galaxies, like the <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy" title="Andromeda Galaxy">Andromeda Galaxy</a>, began resolving them into huge conglomerations of stars, but based simply on the apparent faintness and sheer population of stars, the true distances of these objects placed them well beyond the Milky Way. For this reason they were popularly called <i>island universes</i>, but this term quickly fell into disuse, as the word <i>universe</i> implied the entirety of existence. Instead, they became known simply as galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rao2005_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-rao2005-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomenclature">Nomenclature</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Probing_the_distant_past_SDSS_J1152%2B3313.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1946" data-file-width="3446" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Probing_the_distant_past_SDSS_J1152%2B3313.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Probing_the_distant_past_SDSS_J1152%2B3313.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Probing_the_distant_past_SDSS_J1152%2B3313.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Probing_the_distant_past_SDSS_J1152%2B3313.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Probing_the_distant_past_SDSS_J1152%2B3313.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Probing_the_distant_past_SDSS_J1152%2B3313.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Probing_the_distant_past_SDSS_J1152%2B3313.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Galaxy_cluster" title="Galaxy cluster">Galaxy cluster</a> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=SDSS_J1152%2B3313&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="SDSS J1152+3313 (page does not exist)">SDSS J1152+3313</a>. SDSS stands for <a href="/wiki/Sloan_Digital_Sky_Survey" title="Sloan Digital Sky Survey">Sloan Digital Sky Survey</a>, J for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Julian_epoch" title="Julian epoch">Julian epoch</a>, and 1152+3313 for the <a href="/wiki/Declination" title="Declination">declination</a> and <a href="/wiki/Right_ascension" title="Right ascension">right ascension</a> respectivelly.</div></div></div>
<p>Tens of thousands of galaxies have been catalogued, but only a few have well-established names, such as the <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy" title="Andromeda Galaxy">Andromeda Galaxy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Magellanic_Clouds" title="Magellanic Clouds">Magellanic Clouds</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Whirlpool_Galaxy" title="Whirlpool Galaxy">Whirlpool Galaxy</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Sombrero_Galaxy" title="Sombrero Galaxy">Sombrero Galaxy</a>. Astronomers work with numbers from certain catalogues, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Messier_catalogue" title="Messier catalogue">Messier catalogue</a>, the NGC (<a href="/wiki/New_General_Catalogue" title="New General Catalogue">New General Catalogue</a>), the IC (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Index_Catalogue" title="Index Catalogue">Index Catalogue</a>), the CGCG (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalogue_of_Galaxies_and_of_Clusters_of_Galaxies" title="Catalogue of Galaxies and of Clusters of Galaxies">Catalogue of Galaxies and of Clusters of Galaxies</a>), the MCG (<a href="/wiki/Morphological_Catalogue_of_Galaxies" title="Morphological Catalogue of Galaxies">Morphological Catalogue of Galaxies</a>) and UGC (<a href="/wiki/Uppsala_General_Catalogue" title="Uppsala General Catalogue">Uppsala General Catalogue</a> of Galaxies). All of the well-known galaxies appear in one or more of these catalogues but each time under a different number.
For example, <a href="/wiki/Messier_109" title="Messier 109">Messier 109</a> is a spiral galaxy having the number 109 in the catalogue of Messier, but also codes NGC3992, UGC6937, CGCG 269-023, MCG +09-20-044, and PGC 37617.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observation_history">Observation history</span></h2>
<p>The realization that we live in a galaxy which is one among many galaxies, parallels major discoveries that were made about the Milky Way and other <a href="/wiki/Nebula" title="Nebula">nebulae</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Milky_Way">Milky Way</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_philosophy" title="Greek philosophy">Greek</a> philosopher <a href="/wiki/Democritus" title="Democritus">Democritus</a> (450–370 BCE) proposed that the bright band on the night sky known as the Milky Way might consist of distant stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plutarch_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plutarch-17">[17]</a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> (384–322 BCE), however, believed the Milky Way to be caused by "the ignition of the fiery exhalation of some stars that were large, numerous and close together" and that the "ignition takes place in the upper part of the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Sublunary_sphere" title="Sublunary sphere">region of the World that is continuous with the heavenly motions</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montada_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Montada-18">[18]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonist</a> philosopher <a href="/wiki/Olympiodorus_the_Younger" title="Olympiodorus the Younger">Olympiodorus the Younger</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 495</span>–570 CE) was critical of this view, arguing that if the Milky Way is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sublunary" title="Sublunary">sublunary</a> (situated between Earth and the Moon) it should appear different at different times and places on Earth, and that it should have <a href="/wiki/Parallax" title="Parallax">parallax</a>, which it does not. In his view, the Milky Way is celestial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heidarzadeh23_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-heidarzadeh23-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Mohani Mohamed, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_astronomy" title="Islamic astronomy">Arabian</a> astronomer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alhazen" title="Alhazen">Alhazen</a> (965–1037) made the first attempt at observing and measuring the Milky Way's parallax,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mohamed_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-mohamed-20">[20]</a></sup> and he thus "determined that because the Milky Way had no parallax, it must be remote from the Earth, not belonging to the atmosphere."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persian</a> astronomer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-B%C4%ABr%C5%ABn%C4%AB" title="Al-Bīrūnī">al-Bīrūnī</a> (973–1048) proposed the Milky Way galaxy to be "a collection of countless fragments of the nature of nebulous stars."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-al_biruni_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-al_biruni-23">[23]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Andalusian</a> astronomer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibn_B%C3%A2jjah" title="Ibn Bâjjah">Ibn Bâjjah</a> ("Avempace", <abbr title="died">d.</abbr> 1138) proposed that the Milky Way is made up of many stars that almost touch one another and appear to be a continuous image due to the effect of <a href="/wiki/Refraction" title="Refraction">refraction</a> from sublunary material,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montada_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Montada-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heidarzadeh25_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-heidarzadeh25-24">[24]</a></sup> citing his observation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conjunction_(astronomy_and_astrology)" title="Conjunction (astronomy and astrology)">conjunction</a> of Jupiter and Mars as evidence of this occurring when two objects are near.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montada_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-Montada-18">[18]</a></sup> In the 14th century, the Syrian-born <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibn_Qayyim" title="Ibn Qayyim">Ibn Qayyim</a> proposed the Milky Way galaxy to be "a myriad of tiny stars packed together in the sphere of the fixed stars."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Livingston_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Livingston-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herschel-Galaxy.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="668" decoding="async" height="66" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Herschel-Galaxy.png/220px-Herschel-Galaxy.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Herschel-Galaxy.png/330px-Herschel-Galaxy.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Herschel-Galaxy.png/440px-Herschel-Galaxy.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Herschel-Galaxy.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The shape of the Milky Way as estimated from star counts by <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel" title="William Herschel">William Herschel</a> in 1785; the Solar System was assumed to be near the center.</div></div></div>
<p>Actual proof of the Milky Way consisting of many stars came in 1610 when the Italian astronomer <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> used a <a href="/wiki/Optical_telescope" title="Optical telescope">telescope</a> to study the Milky Way and discovered that it is composed of a huge number of faint stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
In 1750 the English astronomer <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Wright_(astronomer)" title="Thomas Wright (astronomer)">Thomas Wright</a>, in his <i>An original theory or new hypothesis of the Universe</i>, speculated (correctly) that the galaxy might be a rotating body of a huge number of stars held together by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">gravitational</a> forces, akin to the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> but on a much larger scale. The resulting disk of stars can be seen as a band on the sky from our perspective inside the disk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-our_galaxy_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-our_galaxy-29">[29]</a></sup> In a treatise in 1755, <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a> elaborated on Wright's idea about the structure of the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first project to describe the shape of the Milky Way and the position of the Sun was undertaken by <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel" title="William Herschel">William Herschel</a> in 1785 by counting the number of stars in different regions of the sky. He produced a diagram of the shape of the galaxy with <a href="/wiki/Galactocentrism" title="Galactocentrism">the Solar System close to the center</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paul1993_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-paul1993-32">[32]</a></sup> Using a refined approach, <a href="/wiki/Jacobus_Kapteyn" title="Jacobus Kapteyn">Kapteyn</a> in 1920 arrived at the picture of a small (diameter about 15 kiloparsecs) ellipsoid galaxy with the Sun close to the center. A different method by <a href="/wiki/Harlow_Shapley" title="Harlow Shapley">Harlow Shapley</a> based on the cataloguing of <a href="/wiki/Globular_cluster" title="Globular cluster">globular clusters</a> led to a radically different picture: a flat disk with diameter approximately 70 kiloparsecs and the Sun far from the center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-our_galaxy_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-our_galaxy-29">[29]</a></sup> Both analyses failed to take into account the <a href="/wiki/Extinction_(astronomy)" title="Extinction (astronomy)">absorption of light</a> by <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">interstellar dust</a> present in the <a href="/wiki/Galactic_plane" title="Galactic plane">galactic plane</a>, but after <a href="/wiki/Robert_Julius_Trumpler" title="Robert Julius Trumpler">Robert Julius Trumpler</a> quantified this effect in 1930 by studying <a href="/wiki/Open_cluster" title="Open cluster">open clusters</a>, the present picture of our host galaxy, the Milky Way, emerged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:602px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Milky_Way_Arch.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1212" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Milky_Way_Arch.jpg/600px-Milky_Way_Arch.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Milky_Way_Arch.jpg/900px-Milky_Way_Arch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Milky_Way_Arch.jpg/1200px-Milky_Way_Arch.jpg 2x" width="600"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Milky_Way_Arch.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Fisheye_lens" title="Fisheye lens">fish-eye</a> mosaic of the Milky Way arching at a high inclination across the night sky, shot from a dark-sky location in Chile.  The <a href="/wiki/Magellanic_Clouds" title="Magellanic Clouds">Magellanic Clouds</a>, satellite galaxies of the Milky Way, appear near the left edge.</div></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Distinction_from_other_nebulae">Distinction from other nebulae</span></h3>
<p>A few galaxies outside the Milky Way are visible on a dark night to the <a href="/wiki/Naked_eye" title="Naked eye">unaided eye</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy" title="Andromeda Galaxy">Andromeda Galaxy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Large_Magellanic_Cloud" title="Large Magellanic Cloud">Large Magellanic Cloud</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Small_Magellanic_Cloud" title="Small Magellanic Cloud">Small Magellanic Cloud</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Triangulum_Galaxy" title="Triangulum Galaxy">Triangulum Galaxy</a>. In the 10th century, the Persian astronomer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Sufi" title="Al-Sufi">Al-Sufi</a> made the earliest recorded identification of the Andromeda Galaxy, describing it as a "small cloud".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSOG_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSOG-34">[34]</a></sup> In 964, Al-Sufi probably mentioned the Large Magellanic Cloud in his <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Fixed_Stars" title="Book of Fixed Stars">Book of Fixed Stars</a></i> (referring to "Al Bakr of the southern Arabs",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-obspm2_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-obspm2-35">[35]</a></sup> since at a <a href="/wiki/Declination" title="Declination">declination</a> of about 70° south it was not visible where he lived); it was not well known to Europeans until <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Magellan" title="Ferdinand Magellan">Magellan</a>'s voyage in the 16th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-obspm_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-obspm-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-obspm2_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-obspm2-35">[35]</a></sup>  The Andromeda Galaxy was later independently noted by <a href="/wiki/Simon_Marius" title="Simon Marius">Simon Marius</a> in 1612.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSOG_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-NSOG-34">[34]</a></sup>
In 1734, philosopher <a href="/wiki/Emanuel_Swedenborg" title="Emanuel Swedenborg">Emanuel Swedenborg</a> in his <i>Principia</i> speculated that there may be galaxies outside our own that are formed into galactic clusters that are miniscule parts of the universe which extends far beyond what we can see. These views "are remarkably close to the present-day views of the cosmos."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon2002_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gordon2002-37">[37]</a></sup>
In 1750, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Wright_(astronomer)" title="Thomas Wright (astronomer)">Thomas Wright</a> speculated (correctly) that the Milky Way is a flattened disk of stars, and that some of the <a href="/wiki/Nebula" title="Nebula">nebulae</a> visible in the night sky might be separate Milky Ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-our_galaxy_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-our_galaxy-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> In 1755, <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a> used the term "island Universe" to describe these distant nebulae.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pic_iroberts1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="371" data-file-width="569" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Pic_iroberts1.jpg/220px-Pic_iroberts1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Pic_iroberts1.jpg/330px-Pic_iroberts1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Pic_iroberts1.jpg/440px-Pic_iroberts1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pic_iroberts1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Photograph of the "Great Andromeda Nebula" from 1899, later identified as the <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy" title="Andromeda Galaxy">Andromeda Galaxy</a></div></div></div>
<p>Toward the end of the 18th century, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Messier" title="Charles Messier">Charles Messier</a> compiled a <a href="/wiki/Messier_object" title="Messier object">catalog</a> containing the 109 brightest celestial objects having nebulous appearance. Subsequently, William Herschel assembled a catalog of 5,000 nebulae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-our_galaxy_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-our_galaxy-29">[29]</a></sup> In 1845, <a href="/wiki/William_Parsons,_3rd_Earl_of_Rosse" title="William Parsons, 3rd Earl of Rosse">Lord Rosse</a> constructed a new telescope and was able to distinguish between elliptical and spiral nebulae. He also managed to make out individual point sources in some of these nebulae, lending credence to Kant's earlier conjecture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1912, <a href="/wiki/Vesto_Slipher" title="Vesto Slipher">Vesto Slipher</a> made spectrographic studies of the brightest spiral nebulae to determine their composition. Slipher discovered that the spiral nebulae have high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doppler_shift" title="Doppler shift">Doppler shifts</a>, indicating that they are moving at a rate exceeding the velocity of the stars he had measured. He found that the majority of these nebulae are moving away from us.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1917, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heber_Curtis" title="Heber Curtis">Heber Curtis</a> observed nova <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S_Andromedae" title="S Andromedae">S Andromedae</a> within the "Great <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_(constellation)" title="Andromeda (constellation)">Andromeda</a> Nebula" (as the Andromeda Galaxy, <a href="/wiki/Messier_object" title="Messier object">Messier object</a> <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy" title="Andromeda Galaxy">M31</a>, was then known). Searching the photographic record, he found 11 more <a href="/wiki/Nova" title="Nova">novae</a>. Curtis noticed that these novae were, on average, 10 <a href="/wiki/Magnitude_(astronomy)" title="Magnitude (astronomy)">magnitudes</a> fainter than those that occurred within our galaxy. As a result, he was able to come up with a distance estimate of 150,000 <a href="/wiki/Parsec" title="Parsec">parsecs</a>. He became a proponent of the so-called "island universes" hypothesis, which holds that spiral nebulae are actually independent galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1920 a debate took place between <a href="/wiki/Harlow_Shapley" title="Harlow Shapley">Harlow Shapley</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heber_Curtis" title="Heber Curtis">Heber Curtis</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Great_Debate_(astronomy)" title="Great Debate (astronomy)">Great Debate</a>), concerning the nature of the Milky Way, spiral nebulae, and the dimensions of the Universe. To support his claim that the Great Andromeda Nebula is an external galaxy, Curtis noted the appearance of dark lanes resembling the dust clouds in the Milky Way, as well as the significant Doppler shift.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1922, the <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonian</a> astronomer <a href="/wiki/Ernst_%C3%96pik" title="Ernst Öpik">Ernst Öpik</a> gave a distance determination that supported the theory that the Andromeda Nebula is indeed a distant extra-galactic object.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Using the new 100 inch <a href="/wiki/Mount_Wilson_Observatory" title="Mount Wilson Observatory">Mt. Wilson</a> telescope, <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Hubble" title="Edwin Hubble">Edwin Hubble</a> was able to resolve the outer parts of some spiral nebulae as collections of individual stars and identified some <a href="/wiki/Cepheid_variable" title="Cepheid variable">Cepheid variables</a>, thus allowing him to estimate the distance to the nebulae: they were far too distant to be part of the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> In 1936 Hubble produced a classification of <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_morphological_classification" title="Galaxy morphological classification">galactic morphology</a> that is used to this day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_research">Modern research</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GalacticRotation2.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="150" data-file-width="250" decoding="async" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/GalacticRotation2.svg/200px-GalacticRotation2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/GalacticRotation2.svg/300px-GalacticRotation2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/GalacticRotation2.svg/400px-GalacticRotation2.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GalacticRotation2.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Galaxy_rotation_curve" title="Galaxy rotation curve">Rotation curve</a> of a typical spiral galaxy: predicted based on the visible matter (A) and observed (B). The distance is from the <a href="/wiki/Bulge_(astronomy)" title="Bulge (astronomy)">galactic core</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1944, <a href="/wiki/Hendrik_C._van_de_Hulst" title="Hendrik C. van de Hulst">Hendrik van de Hulst</a> predicted that <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwave</a> radiation with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_line" title="Hydrogen line">wavelength of 21 cm</a> would be detectable from interstellar atomic <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> gas;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> and in 1951 it was observed. This radiation is not affected by dust absorption, and so its Doppler shift can be used to map the motion of the gas in our galaxy. These observations led to the hypothesis of a rotating <a href="/wiki/Barred_spiral_galaxy" title="Barred spiral galaxy">bar structure</a> in the center of our galaxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> With improved <a href="/wiki/Radio_telescope" title="Radio telescope">radio telescopes</a>, hydrogen gas could also be traced in other galaxies.
In the 1970s, <a href="/wiki/Vera_Rubin" title="Vera Rubin">Vera Rubin</a> uncovered a discrepancy between observed galactic <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_rotation_curve" title="Galaxy rotation curve">rotation speed</a> and that predicted by the visible mass of stars and gas. Today, the galaxy rotation problem is thought to be explained by the presence of large quantities of unseen <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GOODS_South_field.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="781" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/GOODS_South_field.jpg/220px-GOODS_South_field.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/GOODS_South_field.jpg/330px-GOODS_South_field.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/GOODS_South_field.jpg/440px-GOODS_South_field.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GOODS_South_field.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Scientists used the galaxies visible in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Observatories_Origins_Deep_Survey" title="Great Observatories Origins Deep Survey">GOODS</a> survey to recalculate the total number of galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Beginning in the 1990s, the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> yielded improved observations. Among other things, Hubble data helped establish that the missing dark matter in our galaxy cannot solely consist of inherently faint and small stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Deep_Field" title="Hubble Deep Field">Hubble Deep Field</a>, an extremely long exposure of a relatively empty part of the sky, provided evidence that there are about 125 billion (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7011125000000000000♠</span>1.25<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>11</sup></span>) galaxies in the observable universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Improved technology in detecting the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">spectra</a> invisible to humans (radio telescopes, infrared cameras, and <a href="/wiki/X-ray_astronomy" title="X-ray astronomy">x-ray telescopes</a>) allow detection of other galaxies that are not detected by Hubble. Particularly, galaxy surveys in the <a href="/wiki/Zone_of_Avoidance" title="Zone of Avoidance">Zone of Avoidance</a> (the region of the sky blocked at visible-light wavelengths by the Milky Way) have revealed a number of new galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016, a study published in <a href="/wiki/The_Astrophysical_Journal" title="The Astrophysical Journal">The Astrophysical Journal</a> and led by <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Conselice" title="Christopher Conselice">Christopher Conselice</a> of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Nottingham" title="University of Nottingham">University of Nottingham</a> using 3D modeling of images collected over 20 years by the Hubble Space Telescope concluded that there are over 2 trillion (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7012200000000000000♠</span>2<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>12</sup></span>) galaxies in the observable universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Conselice_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Conselice-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20161017_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20161017-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_and_morphology">Types and morphology</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_morphological_classification" title="Galaxy morphological classification">Galaxy morphological classification</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_sequence_photo.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="443" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Hubble_sequence_photo.png/360px-Hubble_sequence_photo.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Hubble_sequence_photo.png/540px-Hubble_sequence_photo.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Hubble_sequence_photo.png/720px-Hubble_sequence_photo.png 2x" width="360"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_sequence_photo.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Types of galaxies according to the Hubble classification scheme: an <i>E</i> indicates a type of elliptical galaxy; an <i>S</i> is a spiral; and <i>SB</i> is a barred-spiral galaxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[note 1]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Galaxies come in three main types: ellipticals, spirals, and irregulars. A slightly more extensive description of galaxy types based on their appearance is given by the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_sequence" title="Hubble sequence">Hubble sequence</a>. Since the Hubble sequence is entirely based upon visual morphological type (shape), it may miss certain important characteristics of galaxies such as <a href="/wiki/Star_formation" title="Star formation">star formation</a> rate in <a href="/wiki/Starburst_galaxy" title="Starburst galaxy">starburst galaxies</a> and activity in the cores of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Active_galaxy" title="Active galaxy">active galaxies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IRatlas_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-IRatlas-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ellipticals">Ellipticals</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Elliptical_galaxy" title="Elliptical galaxy">Elliptical galaxy</a></div>
<p>The Hubble classification system rates elliptical galaxies on the basis of their ellipticity, ranging from E0, being nearly spherical, up to E7, which is highly elongated. These galaxies have an <a href="/wiki/Ellipsoid" title="Ellipsoid">ellipsoidal</a> profile, giving them an elliptical appearance regardless of the viewing angle. Their appearance shows little structure and they typically have relatively little <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar matter</a>. Consequently, these galaxies also have a low portion of <a href="/wiki/Open_cluster" title="Open cluster">open clusters</a> and a reduced rate of new star formation. Instead they are dominated by generally older, more <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution" title="Stellar evolution">evolved stars</a> that are orbiting the common center of gravity in random directions. The stars contain low abundances of heavy elements because star formation ceases after the initial burst. In this sense they have some similarity to the much smaller <a href="/wiki/Globular_cluster" title="Globular cluster">globular clusters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elliptical_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-elliptical-58">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>The largest galaxies are giant ellipticals. Many elliptical galaxies are believed to form due to the <a href="/wiki/Interacting_galaxy" title="Interacting galaxy">interaction of galaxies</a>, resulting in a collision and merger. They can grow to enormous sizes (compared to spiral galaxies, for example), and giant elliptical galaxies are often found near the core of large galaxy clusters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Shell_galaxy">Shell galaxy</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NGC_3923_Elliptical_Shell_Galaxy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1290" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/NGC_3923_Elliptical_Shell_Galaxy.jpg/220px-NGC_3923_Elliptical_Shell_Galaxy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/NGC_3923_Elliptical_Shell_Galaxy.jpg/330px-NGC_3923_Elliptical_Shell_Galaxy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/NGC_3923_Elliptical_Shell_Galaxy.jpg/440px-NGC_3923_Elliptical_Shell_Galaxy.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NGC_3923_Elliptical_Shell_Galaxy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/NGC_3923" title="NGC 3923">NGC 3923</a> Elliptical Shell Galaxy-Hubble Space Telescope photograph</div></div></div>
<p>A shell galaxy is a type of elliptical galaxy where the stars in the galaxy's halo are arranged in concentric shells. About one-tenth of elliptical galaxies have a shell-like structure, which has never been observed in spiral galaxies. The shell-like structures are thought to develop when a larger galaxy absorbs a smaller companion galaxy. As the two galaxy centers approach, the centers start to oscillate around a center point, the oscillation creates gravitational ripples forming the shells of stars, similar to ripples spreading on water. For example, galaxy <a href="/wiki/NGC_3923" title="NGC 3923">NGC 3923</a> has over twenty shells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spirals">Spirals</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Spiral_galaxy" title="Spiral galaxy">Spiral galaxy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barred_spiral_galaxy" title="Barred spiral galaxy">Barred spiral galaxy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:M101_hires_STScI-PRC2006-10a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="12392" data-file-width="15852" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/M101_hires_STScI-PRC2006-10a.jpg/220px-M101_hires_STScI-PRC2006-10a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/M101_hires_STScI-PRC2006-10a.jpg/330px-M101_hires_STScI-PRC2006-10a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/M101_hires_STScI-PRC2006-10a.jpg/440px-M101_hires_STScI-PRC2006-10a.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:M101_hires_STScI-PRC2006-10a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Pinwheel_Galaxy" title="Pinwheel Galaxy">Pinwheel Galaxy</a>, NGC 5457</div></div></div>
<p>Spiral galaxies resemble spiraling <a href="/wiki/Pinwheel_(toy)" title="Pinwheel (toy)">pinwheels</a>. Though the stars and other visible material contained in such a galaxy lie mostly on a plane, the majority of mass in spiral galaxies exists in a roughly spherical halo of <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a> that extends beyond the visible component, as demonstrated by the universal rotation curve concept.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams2009_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams2009-61">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Spiral galaxies consist of a rotating disk of stars and interstellar medium, along with a central bulge of generally older stars. Extending outward from the <a href="/wiki/Bulge_(astronomy)" title="Bulge (astronomy)">bulge</a> are relatively bright arms. In the Hubble classification scheme, spiral galaxies are listed as type <i>S</i>, followed by a letter (<i>a</i>, <i>b</i>, or <i>c</i>) that indicates the degree of tightness of the spiral arms and the size of the central bulge. An <i>Sa</i> galaxy has tightly wound, poorly defined arms and possesses a relatively large core region. At the other extreme, an <i>Sc</i> galaxy has open, well-defined arms and a small core region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup> A galaxy with poorly defined arms is sometimes referred to as a <a href="/wiki/Flocculent_spiral_galaxy" title="Flocculent spiral galaxy">flocculent spiral galaxy</a>; in contrast to the <a href="/wiki/Grand_design_spiral_galaxy" title="Grand design spiral galaxy">grand design spiral galaxy</a> that has prominent and well-defined spiral arms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bergh1998_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-bergh1998-63">[62]</a></sup> The speed in which a galaxy rotates is thought to correlate with the flatness of the disc as some spiral galaxies have thick bulges, while others are thin and dense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hubble2005-01-barred-spiral-galaxy-NGC1300.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3787" data-file-width="6637" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Hubble2005-01-barred-spiral-galaxy-NGC1300.jpg/300px-Hubble2005-01-barred-spiral-galaxy-NGC1300.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Hubble2005-01-barred-spiral-galaxy-NGC1300.jpg/450px-Hubble2005-01-barred-spiral-galaxy-NGC1300.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Hubble2005-01-barred-spiral-galaxy-NGC1300.jpg/600px-Hubble2005-01-barred-spiral-galaxy-NGC1300.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hubble2005-01-barred-spiral-galaxy-NGC1300.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/NGC_1300" title="NGC 1300">NGC 1300</a>, an example of a <a href="/wiki/Barred_spiral_galaxy" title="Barred spiral galaxy">barred spiral galaxy</a></div></div></div>
<p>In spiral galaxies, the spiral arms do have the shape of approximate <a href="/wiki/Logarithmic_spiral" title="Logarithmic spiral">logarithmic spirals</a>, a pattern that can be theoretically shown to result from a disturbance in a uniformly rotating mass of stars. Like the stars, the spiral arms rotate around the center, but they do so with constant <a href="/wiki/Angular_velocity" title="Angular velocity">angular velocity</a>. The spiral arms are thought to be areas of high-density matter, or "<a href="/wiki/Density_wave_theory" title="Density wave theory">density waves</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bertin_lin1996_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-bertin_lin1996-65">[64]</a></sup> As stars move through an arm, the space velocity of each stellar system is modified by the gravitational force of the higher density. (The velocity returns to normal after the stars depart on the other side of the arm.) This effect is akin to a "wave" of slowdowns moving along a highway full of moving cars. The arms are visible because the high density facilitates star formation, and therefore they harbor many bright and young stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-belkora355_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-belkora355-66">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hoag%27s_object.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1489" data-file-width="1521" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Hoag%27s_object.jpg/220px-Hoag%27s_object.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Hoag%27s_object.jpg/330px-Hoag%27s_object.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Hoag%27s_object.jpg/440px-Hoag%27s_object.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hoag%27s_object.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hoag%27s_Object" title="Hoag's Object">Hoag's Object</a>, an example of a <a href="/wiki/Ring_galaxy" title="Ring galaxy">ring galaxy</a></div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Barred_spiral_galaxy">Barred spiral galaxy</span></h4>
<p>A majority of spiral galaxies, including our own <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> galaxy, have a linear, bar-shaped band of stars that extends outward to either side of the core, then merges into the spiral arm structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> In the Hubble classification scheme, these are designated by an <i>SB</i>, followed by a lower-case letter (<i>a</i>, <i>b</i> or <i>c</i>) that indicates the form of the spiral arms (in the same manner as the categorization of normal spiral galaxies). Bars are thought to be temporary structures that can occur as a result of a density wave radiating outward from the core, or else due to a <a href="/wiki/Galactic_tide" title="Galactic tide">tidal interaction</a> with another galaxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> Many barred spiral galaxies are active, possibly as a result of gas being channeled into the core along the arms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Our own galaxy, the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>, is a large disk-shaped barred-spiral galaxy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup> about 30 kiloparsecs in diameter and a kiloparsec thick. It contains about two hundred billion (2×10<sup>11</sup>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> stars and has a total mass of about six hundred billion (6×10<sup>11</sup>) times the mass of the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Super-luminous_spiral">Super-luminous spiral</span></h4>
<p>Recently, researchers described galaxies called super-luminous spirals. They are very large with an upward diameter of 437,000 light-years (compared to the Milky Way's 100,000 light-year diameter). With a mass of 340 billion solar masses, they generate a significant amount of ultraviolet and mid-infrared light. They are thought to have an increased star formation rate around 30 times faster than the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_morphologies">Other morphologies</span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Peculiar_galaxy" title="Peculiar galaxy">Peculiar galaxies</a> are galactic formations that develop unusual properties due to tidal interactions with other galaxies.
<ul><li>A <a href="/wiki/Ring_galaxy" title="Ring galaxy">ring galaxy</a> has a ring-like structure of stars and interstellar medium surrounding a bare core.  A ring galaxy is thought to occur when a smaller galaxy passes through the core of a spiral galaxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> Such an event may have affected the <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy#Structure" title="Andromeda Galaxy">Andromeda Galaxy</a>, as it displays a multi-ring-like structure when viewed in <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup></li></ul></li>
<li>A <a href="/wiki/Lenticular_galaxy" title="Lenticular galaxy">lenticular galaxy</a> is an intermediate form that has properties of both elliptical and spiral galaxies. These are categorized as Hubble type S0, and they possess ill-defined spiral arms with an elliptical halo of stars<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barred_lenticular_galaxy" title="Barred lenticular galaxy">barred lenticular galaxies</a> receive Hubble classification SB0.)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Irregular_galaxy" title="Irregular galaxy">Irregular galaxies</a> are galaxies that can not be readily classified into an elliptical or spiral morphology.
<ul><li>An Irr-I galaxy has some structure but does not align cleanly with the Hubble classification scheme.</li>
<li>Irr-II galaxies do not possess any structure that resembles a Hubble classification, and may have been disrupted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> Nearby examples of (dwarf) irregular galaxies include the <a href="/wiki/Magellanic_Clouds" title="Magellanic Clouds">Magellanic Clouds</a>.</li></ul></li>
<li>An <a href="/wiki/Ultra_diffuse_galaxy" title="Ultra diffuse galaxy">ultra diffuse galaxy</a> (UDG) is an extremely-low-density galaxy. The galaxy may be the same size as the Milky Way but has a visible star count of only 1% of the Milky Way. The lack of luminosity is because there is a lack of star-forming gas in the galaxy which results in old stellar populations.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dwarfs">Dwarfs</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_galaxy" title="Dwarf galaxy">Dwarf galaxy</a></div>
<p>Despite the prominence of large elliptical and spiral galaxies, most galaxies in the Universe are dwarf galaxies. These galaxies are relatively small when compared with other galactic formations, being about one hundredth the size of the Milky Way, containing only a few billion stars. Ultra-compact dwarf galaxies have recently been discovered that are only 100 parsecs across.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many dwarf galaxies may orbit a single larger galaxy; the Milky Way has at least a dozen such satellites, with an estimated 300–500 yet to be discovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> Dwarf galaxies may also be classified as <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_elliptical_galaxy" title="Dwarf elliptical galaxy">elliptical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_spiral_galaxy" title="Dwarf spiral galaxy">spiral</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Irregular_galaxy" title="Irregular galaxy">irregular</a>. Since small dwarf ellipticals bear little resemblance to large ellipticals, they are often called <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_spheroidal_galaxy" title="Dwarf spheroidal galaxy">dwarf spheroidal galaxies</a> instead.
</p><p>A study of 27 Milky Way neighbors found that in all dwarf galaxies, the central mass is approximately 10 million <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">solar masses</a>, regardless of whether the galaxy has thousands or millions of stars. This has led to the suggestion that galaxies are largely formed by <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a>, and that the minimum size may indicate a form of <a href="/wiki/Warm_dark_matter" title="Warm dark matter">warm dark matter</a> incapable of gravitational coalescence on a smaller scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_types_of_galaxies">Other types of galaxies</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interacting">Interacting</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Interacting_galaxy" title="Interacting galaxy">Interacting galaxy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antennae_galaxies_xl.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3885" data-file-width="3915" decoding="async" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Antennae_galaxies_xl.jpg/200px-Antennae_galaxies_xl.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Antennae_galaxies_xl.jpg/300px-Antennae_galaxies_xl.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Antennae_galaxies_xl.jpg/400px-Antennae_galaxies_xl.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Antennae_galaxies_xl.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Antennae_Galaxies" title="Antennae Galaxies">Antennae Galaxies</a> are undergoing a collision that will result in their eventual merger.</div></div></div>
<p>Interactions between galaxies are relatively frequent, and they can play an important role in <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_formation_and_evolution" title="Galaxy formation and evolution">galactic evolution</a>. Near misses between galaxies result in warping distortions due to <a href="/wiki/Galactic_tide" title="Galactic tide">tidal interactions</a>, and may cause some exchange of gas and dust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-umda_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-umda-82">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suia_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-suia-83">[82]</a></sup>
Collisions occur when two galaxies pass directly through each other and have sufficient relative momentum not to merge. The stars of interacting galaxies will usually not collide, but the gas and dust within the two forms will interact, sometimes triggering star formation. A collision can severely distort the shape of the galaxies, forming bars, rings or tail-like structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-umda_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-umda-82">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suia_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-suia-83">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the extreme of interactions are galactic mergers. In this case the relative momentum of the two galaxies is insufficient to allow the galaxies to pass through each other. Instead, they gradually merge to form a single, larger galaxy. Mergers can result in significant changes to morphology, as compared to the original galaxies. If one of the merging galaxies is much more massive than the other merging galaxy then the result is known as <a href="/wiki/Interacting_galaxy#Galactic_cannibalism" title="Interacting galaxy">cannibalism</a>. The more massive larger galaxy will remain relatively undisturbed by the merger, while the smaller galaxy is torn apart. The Milky Way galaxy is currently in the process of cannibalizing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sagittarius_Dwarf_Elliptical_Galaxy" title="Sagittarius Dwarf Elliptical Galaxy">Sagittarius Dwarf Elliptical Galaxy</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canis_Major_Dwarf_Galaxy" title="Canis Major Dwarf Galaxy">Canis Major Dwarf Galaxy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-umda_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-umda-82">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suia_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-suia-83">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Starburst">Starburst</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Starburst_galaxy" title="Starburst galaxy">Starburst galaxy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:M82_HST_ACS_2006-14-a-large_web.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="7400" data-file-width="9500" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/M82_HST_ACS_2006-14-a-large_web.jpg/200px-M82_HST_ACS_2006-14-a-large_web.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/M82_HST_ACS_2006-14-a-large_web.jpg/300px-M82_HST_ACS_2006-14-a-large_web.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/M82_HST_ACS_2006-14-a-large_web.jpg/400px-M82_HST_ACS_2006-14-a-large_web.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:M82_HST_ACS_2006-14-a-large_web.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Messier_82" title="Messier 82">M82</a>, a starburst galaxy that has ten times the star formation of a "normal" galaxy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Stars are created within galaxies from a reserve of cold gas that forms into giant <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">molecular clouds</a>. Some galaxies have been observed to form stars at an exceptional rate, which is known as a starburst. If they continue to do so, then they would consume their reserve of gas in a time span less than the lifespan of the galaxy. Hence starburst activity usually lasts for only about ten million years, a relatively brief period in the history of a galaxy. Starburst galaxies were more common during the early history of the Universe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chandra_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-chandra-85">[84]</a></sup> and, at present, still contribute an estimated 15% to the total star production rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Starburst galaxies are characterized by dusty concentrations of gas and the appearance of newly formed stars, including massive stars that ionize the surrounding clouds to create <a href="/wiki/H_II_region" title="H II region">H II regions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup> These massive stars produce <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernova</a> explosions, resulting in expanding <a href="/wiki/Supernova_remnant" title="Supernova remnant">remnants</a> that interact powerfully with the surrounding gas. These outbursts trigger a chain reaction of star building that spreads throughout the gaseous region. Only when the available gas is nearly consumed or dispersed does the starburst activity end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chandra_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-chandra-85">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>Starbursts are often associated with merging or interacting galaxies. The prototype example of such a starburst-forming interaction is <a href="/wiki/Messier_82" title="Messier 82">M82</a>, which experienced a close encounter with the larger <a href="/wiki/Messier_81" title="Messier 81">M81</a>. Irregular galaxies often exhibit spaced knots of starburst activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Active_galaxy">Active galaxy</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Active_galactic_nucleus" title="Active galactic nucleus">Active galactic nucleus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:M87_jet.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1276" data-file-width="1222" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/M87_jet.jpg/200px-M87_jet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/M87_jet.jpg/300px-M87_jet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/M87_jet.jpg/400px-M87_jet.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:M87_jet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A jet of particles is being emitted from the core of the elliptical radio galaxy <a href="/wiki/Messier_87" title="Messier 87">M87</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>A portion of the observable galaxies are classified as active galaxies if the galaxy contains an active galactic nucleus (AGN).  A significant portion of the total energy output from the galaxy is emitted by the active galactic nucleus, instead of the stars, dust and <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a> of the galaxy.
</p><p>The standard model for an <a href="/wiki/Active_galactic_nucleus" title="Active galactic nucleus">active galactic nucleus</a> is based upon an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accretion_disc" title="Accretion disc">accretion disc</a> that forms around a <a href="/wiki/Supermassive_black_hole" title="Supermassive black hole">supermassive black hole</a> (SMBH) at the core region of the galaxy. The radiation from an active galactic nucleus results from the <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_energy" title="Gravitational energy">gravitational energy</a> of matter as it falls toward the black hole from the disc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-keel_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-keel-89">[88]</a></sup> In about 10% of these galaxies, a diametrically opposed pair of <a href="/wiki/Astrophysical_jet" title="Astrophysical jet">energetic jets</a> ejects particles from the galaxy core at velocities close to the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>. The mechanism for producing these jets is not well understood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-monster_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-monster-90">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Seyfert_galaxy" title="Seyfert galaxy">Seyfert galaxies</a> or <a href="/wiki/Quasar" title="Quasar">quasars</a>, are classified depending on the luminosity, are active galaxies that emit high-energy radiation in the form of <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">x-rays</a>.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Blazars">Blazars</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Blazar" title="Blazar">Blazar</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Blazar" title="Blazar">Blazars</a> are believed to be an active galaxy with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relativistic_jet" title="Relativistic jet">relativistic jet</a> that is pointed in the direction of Earth. A <a href="/wiki/Radio_galaxy" title="Radio galaxy">radio galaxy</a> emits radio frequencies from relativistic jets. A unified model of these types of active galaxies explains their differences based on the viewing angle of the observer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-monster_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-monster-90">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="LINERS">LINERS</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Low-ionization_nuclear_emission-line_region" title="Low-ionization nuclear emission-line region">Low-ionization nuclear emission-line region</a></div>
<p>Possibly related to active galactic nuclei (as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Starburst_(astronomy)" title="Starburst (astronomy)">starburst</a> regions) are <a href="/wiki/Low-ionization_nuclear_emission-line_region" title="Low-ionization nuclear emission-line region">low-ionization nuclear emission-line regions</a> (LINERs). The emission from LINER-type galaxies is dominated by weakly <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ionized</a> elements. The excitation sources for the weakly ionized lines include post-<a href="/wiki/Asymptotic_giant_branch" title="Asymptotic giant branch">AGB</a> stars, AGN, and shocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heckman1980_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-heckman1980-91">[90]</a></sup> Approximately one-third of nearby galaxies are classified as containing LINER nuclei.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-keel_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-keel-89">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heckman1980_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-heckman1980-91">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hoetal1997b_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-hoetal1997b-92">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Seyfert_galaxy">Seyfert galaxy</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Seyfert_galaxy" title="Seyfert galaxy">Seyfert galaxy</a></div>
<p>Seyfert galaxies are one of the two largest groups of active galaxies, along with quasars. They have quasar-like nuclei (very luminous, distant and bright sources of electromagnetic radiation) with very high surface brightnesses but unlike quasars, their host galaxies are clearly detectable. Seyfert galaxies account for about 10% of all galaxies. Seen in visible light, most Seyfert galaxies look like normal spiral galaxies, but when studied under other wavelengths, the luminosity of their cores is equivalent to the luminosity of whole galaxies the size of the Milky Way.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Quasar">Quasar</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Quasar" title="Quasar">Quasar</a></div>
<p>Quasars (/ˈkweɪzɑr/) or quasi-stellar radio sources are the most energetic and distant members of active galactic nuclei. Quasars are extremely luminous and were first identified as being high redshift sources of electromagnetic energy, including radio waves and visible light, that appeared to be similar to stars, rather than extended sources similar to galaxies. Their luminosity can be 100 times greater than that of the Milky Way.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Luminous_infrared_galaxy">Luminous infrared galaxy</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Luminous_infrared_galaxy" title="Luminous infrared galaxy">Luminous infrared galaxy</a></div>
<p>Luminous infrared galaxies or LIRGs are galaxies with luminosities, the measurement of brightness, above 10<sup>11</sup> L☉. LIRGs are more abundant than starburst galaxies, Seyfert galaxies and quasi-stellar objects at comparable total luminosity. Infrared galaxies emit more energy in the infrared than at all other wavelengths combined. A LIRG's luminosity is 100 billion times that of our Sun.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties">Properties</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_fields">Magnetic fields</span></h3>
<p>Galaxies have <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic fields</a> of their own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-galactic_magnetic_fields_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-galactic_magnetic_fields-93">[92]</a></sup> They are strong enough to be dynamically important: they drive mass inflow into the centers of galaxies, they modify the formation of spiral arms and they can affect the rotation of gas in the outer regions of galaxies. Magnetic fields provide the transport of angular momentum required for the collapse of gas clouds and hence the formation of new stars.
</p><p>The typical average equipartition strength for <a href="/wiki/Spiral_galaxy" title="Spiral galaxy">spiral galaxies</a> is about 10 μG (<a href="/wiki/Gauss_(unit)" title="Gauss (unit)">microGauss</a>) or 1 nT (<a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">nanoTesla</a>). For comparison, the Earth's magnetic field has an average strength of about 0.3 G (Gauss or 30 μT (<a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">microTesla</a>). Radio-faint galaxies like <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy" title="Andromeda Galaxy">M 31</a> and <a href="/wiki/Triangulum_Galaxy" title="Triangulum Galaxy">M 33</a>, our <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>'s neighbors, have weaker fields (about 5 μG), while gas-rich galaxies with high star-formation rates, like M 51, M 83 and NGC 6946, have 15 μG on average. In prominent spiral arms the field strength can be up to 25 μG, in regions where cold gas and dust are also concentrated. The strongest total equipartition fields (50–100 μG) were found in <a href="/wiki/Starburst_galaxy" title="Starburst galaxy">starburst galaxies</a>, for example in M 82 and the <a href="/wiki/Antennae_Galaxies" title="Antennae Galaxies">Antennae</a>, and in nuclear starburst regions, for example in the centers of NGC 1097 and of other <a href="/wiki/Barred_spiral_galaxy" title="Barred spiral galaxy">barred galaxies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-galactic_magnetic_fields_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-galactic_magnetic_fields-93">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_and_evolution">Formation and evolution</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_formation_and_evolution" title="Galaxy formation and evolution">Galaxy formation and evolution</a></div>
<p>Galactic formation and evolution is an active area of research in <a href="/wiki/Astrophysics" title="Astrophysics">astrophysics</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Artist%27s_impression_of_a_protocluster_forming_in_the_early_Universe.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="816" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Artist%27s_impression_of_a_protocluster_forming_in_the_early_Universe.jpg/220px-Artist%27s_impression_of_a_protocluster_forming_in_the_early_Universe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Artist%27s_impression_of_a_protocluster_forming_in_the_early_Universe.jpg/330px-Artist%27s_impression_of_a_protocluster_forming_in_the_early_Universe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Artist%27s_impression_of_a_protocluster_forming_in_the_early_Universe.jpg/440px-Artist%27s_impression_of_a_protocluster_forming_in_the_early_Universe.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Artist%27s_impression_of_a_protocluster_forming_in_the_early_Universe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist's impression of a protocluster forming in the early Universe<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Current cosmological models of the early Universe are based on the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a> theory. About 300,000 years after this event, atoms of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> began to form, in an event called <a href="/wiki/Recombination_(cosmology)" title="Recombination (cosmology)">recombination</a>. Nearly all the hydrogen was neutral (non-ionized) and readily absorbed light, and no stars had yet formed. As a result, this period has been called the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Big_Bang#Dark_Ages" title="Timeline of the Big Bang">dark ages</a>". It was from density fluctuations (or <a href="/wiki/Anisotropy" title="Anisotropy">anisotropic</a> irregularities) in this primordial matter that <a href="/wiki/Structure_formation" title="Structure formation">larger structures</a> began to appear.
