As a result, masses of <a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">baryonic</a> matter started to condense within <a href="/wiki/Cold_dark_matter" title="Cold dark matter">cold dark matter</a> halos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hqrdvj_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-hqrdvj-95">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rmaa17_107_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-rmaa17_107-96">[95]</a></sup> These primordial structures would eventually become the galaxies we see today.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Young_Galaxy_Accreting_Material.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Young_Galaxy_Accreting_Material.jpg/200px-Young_Galaxy_Accreting_Material.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Young_Galaxy_Accreting_Material.jpg/300px-Young_Galaxy_Accreting_Material.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Young_Galaxy_Accreting_Material.jpg/400px-Young_Galaxy_Accreting_Material.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Young_Galaxy_Accreting_Material.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist's impression of a young galaxy accreting material</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_galaxies">Early galaxies</span></h4>
<p>Evidence for the early appearance of galaxies was found in 2006, when it was discovered that the galaxy <a href="/wiki/IOK-1" title="IOK-1">IOK-1</a> has an unusually high <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshift</a> of 6.96, corresponding to just 750 million years after the Big Bang and making it the most distant and primordial galaxy yet seen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup>
While some scientists have claimed other objects (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galaxy_Abell_1835_IR1916" title="Galaxy Abell 1835 IR1916">Abell 1835 IR1916</a>) have higher redshifts (and therefore are seen in an earlier stage of the Universe's evolution), IOK-1's age and composition have been more reliably established.  In December 2012, astronomers reported that <a href="/wiki/UDFj-39546284" title="UDFj-39546284">UDFj-39546284</a> is the most distant object known and has a redshift value of 11.9.  The object, estimated to have existed around "380 million years"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-20121212_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space-20121212-98">[97]</a></sup> after the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a> (which was about 13.8 billion years ago),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cosmic_Detectives_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cosmic_Detectives-99">[98]</a></sup> is about 13.42 billion <a href="/wiki/Distance_measures_(cosmology)" title="Distance measures (cosmology)">light travel distance years</a> away. The existence of such early <a href="/wiki/Protogalaxy" title="Protogalaxy">protogalaxies</a> suggests that they must have grown in the so-called "dark ages".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hqrdvj_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-hqrdvj-95">[94]</a></sup> As of May 5, 2015, the galaxy <a href="/wiki/EGS-zs8-1" title="EGS-zs8-1">EGS-zs8-1</a> is the most distant and earliest galaxy measured, forming 670 million years after the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>. The light from EGS-zs8-1 has taken 13 billion years to reach Earth, and is now 30 billion light-years away, because of the <a href="/wiki/Expansion_of_the_universe" title="Expansion of the universe">expansion of the universe</a> during 13 billion years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phys.org_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-phys.org-102">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phys.org_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-phys.org-102">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_galaxy_formation">Early galaxy formation</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Signatures_of_the_Earliest_Galaxies.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="440" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="76" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Signatures_of_the_Earliest_Galaxies.jpg/220px-Signatures_of_the_Earliest_Galaxies.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Signatures_of_the_Earliest_Galaxies.jpg/330px-Signatures_of_the_Earliest_Galaxies.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Signatures_of_the_Earliest_Galaxies.jpg/440px-Signatures_of_the_Earliest_Galaxies.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Signatures_of_the_Earliest_Galaxies.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Different components of near-infrared background light detected by the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> in deep-sky surveys<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The detailed process by which early galaxies formed is an open question in astrophysics. Theories can be divided into two categories: top-down and bottom-up. In top-down correlations (such as the Eggen–Lynden-Bell–Sandage [ELS] model), protogalaxies form in a large-scale simultaneous collapse lasting about one hundred million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup> In bottom-up theories (such as the Searle-Zinn [SZ] model), small structures such as <a href="/wiki/Globular_cluster" title="Globular cluster">globular clusters</a> form first, and then a number of such bodies accrete to form a larger galaxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>Once protogalaxies began to form and contract, the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halo_star" title="Halo star">halo stars</a> (called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Population_3_stars" title="Population 3 stars">Population III stars</a>) appeared within them. These were composed almost entirely of hydrogen and helium, and may have been massive. If so, these huge stars would have quickly consumed their supply of fuel and became <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernovae</a>, releasing heavy elements into the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup> This first generation of stars re-ionized the surrounding neutral hydrogen, creating expanding bubbles of space through which light could readily travel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>In June 2015, astronomers reported evidence for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Population_3_stars" title="Population 3 stars">Population III stars</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Cosmos_Redshift_7" title="Cosmos Redshift 7">Cosmos Redshift 7</a> galaxy at <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i> = 6.60</span>. Such stars are likely to have existed in the very early universe (i.e., at high redshift), and may have started the production of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical elements</a> heavier than <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> that are needed for the later formation of <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> as we know it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AJ-20150604_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-AJ-20150604-110">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150617_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150617-111">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span></h3>
<p>Within a billion years of a galaxy's formation, key structures begin to appear. <a href="/wiki/Globular_cluster" title="Globular cluster">Globular clusters</a>, the central supermassive black hole, and a <a href="/wiki/Bulge_(astronomy)" title="Bulge (astronomy)">galactic bulge</a> of metal-poor <a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">Population II stars</a> form. The creation of a supermassive black hole appears to play a key role in actively regulating the growth of galaxies by limiting the total amount of additional matter added.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup> During this early epoch, galaxies undergo a major burst of star formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the following two billion years, the accumulated matter settles into a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disc_(galaxy)" title="Disc (galaxy)">galactic disc</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup> A galaxy will continue to absorb infalling material from <a href="/wiki/High-velocity_cloud" title="High-velocity cloud">high-velocity clouds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_galaxy" title="Dwarf galaxy">dwarf galaxies</a> throughout its life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> This matter is mostly hydrogen and helium. The cycle of stellar birth and death slowly increases the abundance of heavy elements, eventually allowing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_formation" title="Planetary formation">formation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div style="clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;background-color:transparent"><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field" title="Hubble Extreme Deep Field">Hubble eXtreme Deep Field (XDF)</a></i></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:XDF-scale.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2489" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/XDF-scale.jpg/200px-XDF-scale.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/XDF-scale.jpg/300px-XDF-scale.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/XDF-scale.jpg/400px-XDF-scale.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field" title="Hubble Extreme Deep Field">XDF</a></i> view field compared to the <a href="/wiki/Angular_diameter" title="Angular diameter">angular size</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>. Several thousand galaxies, each consisting of billions of <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, are in this small view.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Constellation_Fornax,_EXtreme_Deep_Field.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2078" data-file-width="2382" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Constellation_Fornax%2C_EXtreme_Deep_Field.jpg/200px-Constellation_Fornax%2C_EXtreme_Deep_Field.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Constellation_Fornax%2C_EXtreme_Deep_Field.jpg/300px-Constellation_Fornax%2C_EXtreme_Deep_Field.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Constellation_Fornax%2C_EXtreme_Deep_Field.jpg/400px-Constellation_Fornax%2C_EXtreme_Deep_Field.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field" title="Hubble Extreme Deep Field">XDF</a></i> (2012) view: Each light speck is a galaxy, some of which are as old as 13.2 billion years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space-20120925_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space-20120925-117">[116]</a></sup> – the <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">observable universe</a> is estimated to contain 200 billion to 2 trillion galaxies.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:XDF-separated.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2101" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/XDF-separated.jpg/200px-XDF-separated.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/XDF-separated.jpg/300px-XDF-separated.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/XDF-separated.jpg/400px-XDF-separated.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field" title="Hubble Extreme Deep Field">XDF</a></i> image shows (from left) fully mature galaxies, nearly mature galaxies (from 5 to 9 billion years ago), and <a href="/wiki/Protogalaxy" title="Protogalaxy">protogalaxies</a>, blazing with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Young_star" title="Young star">young stars</a> (beyond 9 billion years).</div></div></div></div>
<p>The evolution of galaxies can be significantly affected by interactions and collisions. Mergers of galaxies were common during the early epoch, and the majority of galaxies were peculiar in morphology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sa296_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-sa296-118">[117]</a></sup> Given the distances between the stars, the great majority of stellar systems in colliding galaxies will be unaffected. However, gravitational stripping of the interstellar gas and dust that makes up the spiral arms produces a long train of stars known as tidal tails. Examples of these formations can be seen in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NGC_4676" title="NGC 4676">NGC 4676</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup> or the <a href="/wiki/Antennae_Galaxies" title="Antennae Galaxies">Antennae Galaxies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Milky Way galaxy and the nearby Andromeda Galaxy are moving toward each other at about 130 <a href="/wiki/Metre_per_second" title="Metre per second">km/s</a>, and—depending upon the lateral movements—the two might collide in about five to six billion years. Although the Milky Way has never collided with a galaxy as large as Andromeda before, evidence of past collisions of the Milky Way with smaller dwarf galaxies is increasing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>Such large-scale interactions are rare. As time passes, mergers of two systems of equal size become less common. Most bright galaxies have remained fundamentally unchanged for the last few billion years, and the net rate of star formation probably also peaked approximately ten billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_trends">Future trends</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Future_of_an_expanding_universe" title="Future of an expanding universe">Future of an expanding universe</a></div>
<p>Spiral galaxies, like the Milky Way, produce new generations of stars as long as they have dense <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">molecular clouds</a> of interstellar hydrogen in their spiral arms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup> Elliptical galaxies are largely devoid of this gas, and so form few new stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup> The supply of star-forming material is finite; once stars have converted the available supply of hydrogen into heavier elements, new star formation will come to an end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cosmic_battle_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-cosmic_battle-125">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>The current era of star formation is expected to continue for up to one hundred billion years, and then the "stellar age" will wind down after about ten trillion to one hundred trillion years (10<sup>13</sup>–10<sup>14</sup> years), as the smallest, longest-lived stars in our universe, tiny <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">red dwarfs</a>, begin to fade. At the end of the stellar age, galaxies will be composed of <a href="/wiki/Compact_star" title="Compact star">compact objects</a>: <a href="/wiki/Brown_dwarf" title="Brown dwarf">brown dwarfs</a>, <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarfs</a> that are cooling or cold ("<a href="/wiki/Black_dwarf" title="Black dwarf">black dwarfs</a>"), <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron stars</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black holes</a>. Eventually, as a result of <a href="/wiki/Relaxation_(physics)#Relaxation_in_astronomy" title="Relaxation (physics)">gravitational relaxation</a>, all stars will either fall into central supermassive black holes or be flung into intergalactic space as a result of collisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cosmic_battle_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-cosmic_battle-125">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Larger-scale_structures">Larger-scale structures</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe#Large-scale_structure" title="Observable universe">Observable universe § Large-scale structure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_filament" title="Galaxy filament">Galaxy filament</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_groups_and_clusters" title="Galaxy groups and clusters">Galaxy groups and clusters</a></div>
<p>Deep sky surveys show that galaxies are often found in groups and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clusters_of_galaxies" title="Clusters of galaxies">clusters</a>. Solitary galaxies that have not significantly interacted with another galaxy of comparable mass during the past billion years are relatively scarce. Only about 5% of the galaxies surveyed have been found to be truly isolated; however, these isolated formations may have interacted and even merged with other galaxies in the past, and may still be orbited by smaller, satellite galaxies. Isolated galaxies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[note 2]</a></sup> can produce stars at a higher rate than normal, as their gas is not being stripped by other nearby galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the largest scale, the Universe is continually expanding, resulting in an average increase in the separation between individual galaxies (see <a href="/wiki/Hubble%27s_law" title="Hubble's law">Hubble's law</a>). Associations of galaxies can overcome this expansion on a local scale through their mutual gravitational attraction. These associations formed early in the Universe, as clumps of dark matter pulled their respective galaxies together. Nearby groups later merged to form larger-scale clusters. This on-going merger process (as well as an influx of infalling gas) heats the inter-galactic gas within a cluster to very high temperatures, reaching 30–100 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megakelvin" title="Megakelvin">megakelvins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[128]</a></sup> About 70–80% of the mass in a cluster is in the form of dark matter, with 10–30% consisting of this heated gas and the remaining few percent of the matter in the form of galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:234px;max-width:234px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:232px;max-width:232px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Seyfert_Sextet_full.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1405" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Seyfert_Sextet_full.jpg/230px-Seyfert_Sextet_full.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Seyfert_Sextet_full.jpg/345px-Seyfert_Sextet_full.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Seyfert_Sextet_full.jpg/460px-Seyfert_Sextet_full.jpg 2x" width="230"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Seyfert%27s_Sextet" title="Seyfert's Sextet">Seyfert's Sextet</a> is an example of a compact galaxy group.</div></div></div></div>
<p>Most galaxies in the Universe are gravitationally bound to a number of other galaxies. These form a <a href="/wiki/Fractal" title="Fractal">fractal</a>-like hierarchical distribution of clustered structures, with the smallest such associations being termed groups. A group of galaxies is the most common type of galactic cluster, and these formations contain a majority of the galaxies (as well as most of the <a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">baryonic</a> mass) in the Universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[131]</a></sup> To remain gravitationally bound to such a group, each member galaxy must have a sufficiently low velocity to prevent it from escaping (see <a href="/wiki/Virial_theorem" title="Virial theorem">Virial theorem</a>). If there is insufficient <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a>, however, the group may evolve into a smaller number of galaxies through mergers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>Clusters of galaxies consist of hundreds to thousands of galaxies bound together by gravity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hubble_protocluster_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hubble_protocluster-135">[133]</a></sup> Clusters of galaxies are often dominated by a single giant elliptical galaxy, known as the <a href="/wiki/Brightest_cluster_galaxy" title="Brightest cluster galaxy">brightest cluster galaxy</a>, which, over time, <a href="/wiki/Tidal_force" title="Tidal force">tidally</a> destroys its satellite galaxies and adds their mass to its own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Supercluster" title="Supercluster">Superclusters</a> contain tens of thousands of galaxies, which are found in clusters, groups and sometimes individually. At the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Large-scale_structure_of_the_Cosmos" title="Large-scale structure of the Cosmos">supercluster scale</a>, galaxies are arranged into sheets and filaments surrounding vast empty voids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[135]</a></sup> Above this scale, the Universe appears to be the same in all directions (<a href="/wiki/Isotropy" title="Isotropy">isotropic</a> and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Homogeneity" title="wikt:Homogeneity">homogeneous</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Milky Way galaxy is a member of an association named the <a href="/wiki/Local_Group" title="Local Group">Local Group</a>, a relatively small group of galaxies that has a diameter of approximately one megaparsec. The Milky Way and the Andromeda Galaxy are the two brightest galaxies within the group; many of the other member galaxies are dwarf companions of these two galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[137]</a></sup> The Local Group itself is a part of a cloud-like structure within the <a href="/wiki/Virgo_Supercluster" title="Virgo Supercluster">Virgo Supercluster</a>, a large, extended structure of groups and clusters of galaxies centered on the <a href="/wiki/Virgo_Cluster" title="Virgo Cluster">Virgo Cluster</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tully1982_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-tully1982-140">[138]</a></sup> And the Virgo Supercluster itself is a part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pisces-Cetus_Supercluster_Complex" title="Pisces-Cetus Supercluster Complex">Pisces-Cetus Supercluster Complex</a>, a giant <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_filament" title="Galaxy filament">galaxy filament</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Multi-wavelength_observation">Multi-wavelength observation</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Observational_astronomy" title="Observational astronomy">Observational astronomy</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Andromeda_galaxy.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="6200" data-file-width="6200" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Andromeda_galaxy.jpg/220px-Andromeda_galaxy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Andromeda_galaxy.jpg/330px-Andromeda_galaxy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Andromeda_galaxy.jpg/440px-Andromeda_galaxy.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">This ultraviolet image of <a href="/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy" title="Andromeda Galaxy">Andromeda</a> shows blue regions containing young, massive stars.</div></div></div></div>
<p>The peak radiation of most stars lies in the <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">visible spectrum</a>, so the observation of the stars that form galaxies has been a major component of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_astronomy" title="Optical astronomy">optical astronomy</a>. It is also a favorable portion of the spectrum for observing ionized <a href="/wiki/H_II_region" title="H II region">H II regions</a>, and for examining the distribution of dusty arms.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">dust</a> present in the interstellar medium is opaque to visual light. It is more transparent to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Far_infrared_astronomy" title="Far infrared astronomy">far-infrared</a>, which can be used to observe the interior regions of giant molecular clouds and <a href="/wiki/Bulge_(astronomy)" title="Bulge (astronomy)">galactic cores</a> in great detail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[139]</a></sup> Infrared is also used to observe distant, <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">red-shifted</a> galaxies that were formed much earlier in the history of the Universe. Water vapor and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> absorb a number of useful portions of the infrared spectrum, so high-altitude or space-based telescopes are used for <a href="/wiki/Infrared_astronomy" title="Infrared astronomy">infrared astronomy</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_southern_plane_of_the_Milky_Way_from_the_ATLASGAL_survey.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/The_southern_plane_of_the_Milky_Way_from_the_ATLASGAL_survey.jpg/220px-The_southern_plane_of_the_Milky_Way_from_the_ATLASGAL_survey.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/The_southern_plane_of_the_Milky_Way_from_the_ATLASGAL_survey.jpg/330px-The_southern_plane_of_the_Milky_Way_from_the_ATLASGAL_survey.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/The_southern_plane_of_the_Milky_Way_from_the_ATLASGAL_survey.jpg/440px-The_southern_plane_of_the_Milky_Way_from_the_ATLASGAL_survey.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_southern_plane_of_the_Milky_Way_from_the_ATLASGAL_survey.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The southern plane of the Milky Way from submillimeter wavelengths<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[140]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The first non-visual study of galaxies, particularly active galaxies, was made using <a href="/wiki/Radio_astronomy" title="Radio astronomy">radio frequencies</a>. The Earth's atmosphere is nearly transparent to radio between 5 <a href="/wiki/Hertz" title="Hertz">MHz</a> and 30 GHz. (The <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">ionosphere</a> blocks signals below this range.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[141]</a></sup> Large radio <a href="/wiki/Interferometry" title="Interferometry">interferometers</a> have been used to map the active jets emitted from active nuclei. <a href="/wiki/Radio_telescope" title="Radio telescope">Radio telescopes</a> can also be used to observe neutral hydrogen (via <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_line" title="Hydrogen line">21 cm radiation</a>), including, potentially, the non-ionized matter in the early Universe that later collapsed to form galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[142]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UV_astronomy" title="UV astronomy">Ultraviolet</a> and <a href="/wiki/X-ray_astronomy" title="X-ray astronomy">X-ray telescopes</a> can observe highly energetic galactic phenomena. Ultraviolet flares are sometimes observed when a star in a distant galaxy is torn apart from the tidal forces of a nearby black hole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[143]</a></sup> The distribution of hot gas in galactic clusters can be mapped by X-rays. The existence of supermassive black holes at the cores of galaxies was confirmed through X-ray astronomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[144]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-57"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-57">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Galaxies to the left side of the Hubble classification scheme are sometimes referred to as "early-type", while those to the right are "late-type".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-128"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-128">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The term "field galaxy" is sometimes used to mean an isolated galaxy, although the same term is also used to describe galaxies that do not belong to a cluster but may be a member of a group of galaxies.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-28">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Thomas Wright, <i>An Original Theory or New Hypothesis of the Universe</i> … (London, England:  H. Chapelle, 1750).  <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=80VZAAAAcAAJ&amp;pg=PA48" rel="nofollow">From p.48:</a>  " … the stars are not infinitely dispersed and distributed in a promiscuous manner throughout all the mundane space, without order or design, …  this phænomenon [is] no other than a certain effect arising from the observer's situation, …  To a spectator placed in an indefinite space, … it [i.e., the Milky Way (<i>Via Lactea</i>)] [is] a vast ring of stars … "<br/>
<a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=80VZAAAAcAAJ&amp;pg=PA73" rel="nofollow">On page 73</a>, Wright called the Milky Way the <i>Vortex Magnus</i> (the great whirlpool) and estimated its diameter at 8.64×10<sup>12</sup> miles (13.9×10<sup>12</sup> km).</span>
